But for n=5, total points=15, max sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most how many points? In general, for a given slope s (not 0, ∞, -1), the maximum number of integer points in T on a line of that slope is about n/|s+1|? Actually more systematic: A line with slope s (real) that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 will intersect the integer lattice in a set of points spaced by (1, s) if s is rational? But s could be irrational; then it contains at most one integer point. So to maximize coverage, we choose rational slopes with small denominator? For integer lattice points, a line with rational slope p/q (in lowest terms) contains integer points at regular intervals of (q,p). The number of such points in the triangle is at most about n/(|p|+|q|) maybe. The maximum occurs when p/q is as "flat" as possible but not horizontal or slope -1. Slope 1 gives p=q=1, then number of points is floor((n+1)/2) or something. For n=5, slope 1 line y=x+c: c=0 gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) because (4,4) sum=8>6? Actually a+b≤6: for x=4, y=4 => sum=8>6, so only (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points. So slope 1 sunny line can cover up to 3 points. For n=4, slope 1 gave 2 points; for n=5, 3 points. So the maximum points per sunny line increases with n. In general, for a line of slope 1 (or any slope that is not excluded), the maximum number of integer points in T is the number of integer x such that x≥1, x+c≥1, and 2x+c≤n+1, i.e., x ≤ (n+1-c)/2. For c=0, x ≤ (n+1)/2, so count = floor((n+1)/2). So for n odd, floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2; for n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2. So for n=5, (n+1)/2=3. So slope 1 sunny line can cover 3 points. Similarly, slope 2: y=2x+c, integer points require x such that 2x+c≥1 and 3x+c≤n+1. For c=0, x≤(n+1)/3, so count = floor((n+1)/3). For n=5, floor(6/3)=2. So slope 2 gives 2 points. So the maximum per sunny line is about n/2 for slope 1.

We can think of covering T by 3 lines of types H,V,D and then the sunny lines cover the rest. The sunny lines can be any slopes, but they can also cover some points already covered by non-sunny lines. Since the sunny lines are arbitrary (but must be sunny), we can choose them to cover exactly those points, as long as those points are collinear with a sunny line or can be covered by two sunny lines.

Thus the key is to find if there exists a set of 3 non-sunny lines (each H,V,D) such that the uncovered points (those not on any of these 3 lines) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Maybe we can derive necessary conditions on k using combinatorial arguments.

Consider the covering by n lines. We can think about the "boundary" points: points with a=1, b=1, or a+b=n+1. These are on the boundary of the triangle. For each such boundary point, it must lie on at least one line. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal, so they can cover boundary points in a natural way.

We might consider the set of lines that are not sunny; they are axis-aligned or diagonal. Sunny lines are "general" slopes. The points in T are many. There might be a parity or counting argument that limits k.

Another perspective: We can view the problem as a covering of the integer points in a right triangle by lines. Each line can be represented by its equation. We want to know for which k there exists a set of n lines covering all points, with exactly k sunny lines.

Maybe we can construct for any k from 0 to n-2? For n=5, k=0,1,2,3 work; n=4, k=0,1,3 work (k=2 fails); n=3, k=0,1 work. So it's not simply all k up to n-2. For n=4, k=2 fails. So there is some gap.

Given the pattern, we should try to derive a general classification.

From small n, we have:

n=3: k=0,1
n=4: k=0,1,3
n=5: k=0,1,2,3

Actually for fixed sum s, points with a,b≥1, a+b=s: there are s-1 points. So for s from 2 to n+1, the number of points is s-1. So line x+y=s covers s-1 points. So each non-sunny line covers at most n points, but the total points are about n^2/2, which is much larger than n for large n. So we need many lines that cover many points. Actually for a line with rational slope p/q in reduced form, the lattice points are spaced with step (q,p). The number of integer points in the triangle is about n/|p+q|? Not exactly. Thus if we want to cover all points, we might need to use many non-sunny lines because they can cover more points per line. However, we have exactly n lines. The total number of points is n(n+1)/2. If we use only non-sunny lines (k=0), we can cover all points with n lines (e.g., n vertical lines). So that works. If we introduce sunny lines, we might need to compensate by using more non-sunny lines, but we have fixed n lines total. So if we replace a non-sunny line with a sunny line, we might lose coverage, and might need to adjust other lines. So the question is: for which k can we have n lines with exactly k sunny ones covering all points?

Maybe the maximum k is something like floor(n/2)? We need to explore constraints.

We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. Let's denote the triangular set T.

We have n lines. Let's denote the lines L1,...,Ln. Each line is either non-sunny (type H: horizontal, V: vertical, D: diagonal x+y=c) or sunny (other slopes). We need to cover each point.

Observation: The set of points on a line is a set of integer points that are collinear. The triangle T has a natural "boundary" consisting of three sides: the vertical line x=1? Actually the triangle T: points with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. The boundary consists of: the line x=1 (a=1) from b=1 to b=n, the line y=1 (b=1) from a=1 to a=n, and the line a+b=n+1 from a=1 to a=n. But these are lines that contain points of T. However, we can use lines that are not necessarily boundaries.

Maybe we can think about covering points by lines in a combinatorial way: each point must lie on at least one line. So we can assign each point to a line that passes through it. For each line, consider the set of points it covers. The lines are distinct.

We need to find necessary conditions on k. Then we need to construct configurations for all possible k that satisfy the condition.

Let's try small n to get pattern.

n=3: Then T = {(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1)}. That's 6 points. We have 3 lines. Need to cover all 6 points with 3 lines. Now can we have k=1? That means exactly one sunny line, and two non-sunny lines. We need to cover all 6 points. Is it possible? Let's try. The non-sunny lines can be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. The sunny line can have any slope other than 0, undefined, -1. Let's attempt to construct.

We need to cover all 6 points. The points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1).

For integer lattice points, a line with rational slope p/q (in lowest terms) contains integer points at regular intervals of (q,p). So the maximum points per sunny line increases with n. In general, for a line of slope 1 (or any slope that is not excluded), the maximum number of integer points in T is the number of integer x such that x≥1, x+c≥1, and 2x+c≤n+1, i.e., x ≤ (n+1-c)/2. For c=0, x ≤ (n+1)/2, so count = floor((n+1)/2). So for n odd, floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2; for n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2. So for n=5, (n+1)/2=3. So slope 1 sunny line can cover 3 points. Similarly, slope 2: y=2x+c, integer points require x such that 2x+c≥1 and 3x+c≤n+1. For c=0, x≤(n+1)/3, so count = floor((n+1)/3). For n=5, floor(6/3)=2. So slope 2 gives 2 points. So the maximum per sunny line is about n/2 for slope 1.

Let's compute the maximum possible number of points covered by 5 sunny lines (any slopes) in T. Since sunny lines are not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, the maximum points on a sunny line in T is at most floor((n+1)/2) = 3 for n=5, but that's only for slope 1 lines. Actually any point (a,b) in T has a+b≤6, so the line y=x+(b-a) is a slope 1 line. So every point lies on some slope 1 line (the one with c=b-a). So the set of all slope 1 lines covers all points. But we are only allowed to use 5 lines. The maximum number of points that can be covered by 5 slope 1 lines (each covering up to 3 points) is achieved if we choose 5 lines that are as disjoint as possible. But the lines are all of the form y=x+c, and they cover disjoint sets of points when c differ by at least something? Actually for a given point (a,b), its c = b-a. So the set of points with a given c are those with b-a = c. These points are on a line of slope 1. So different c's correspond to disjoint sets of points (since if b-a=c, then c is uniquely determined). So the lines y=x+c partition the integer lattice into parallel lines. We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For any n≥3, k=0 works (e.g., n vertical lines).
- For any n≥3, k=1 works (take horizontals y=1,...,n-1 covering all but (1,n); then a sunny line through (1,n) works).
- For n=3, k=2 fails; n=4, k=2 fails; n=5, k=4 fails; n=6, k=4 fails; n=7, k=4 fails; etc. The pattern suggests that k=2 is possible only when n is not too small? Actually from examples: n=5 k=2 works, n=6 k=2 fails, n=7 k=2? We need to check n=7 k=2: total lines=7, so if k=2, then m=5 non-sunny lines. The maximum union from 5 lines is 7+6+5+4+3=25 (using horizontals y=1..5). That leaves 3 uncovered points: (1,6),(1,7),(2,6) etc. Are they collinear? (1,6)-(1,7) vertical, (1,6)-(2,6) horizontal, (1,7)-(2,6) slope -1, not collinear. So that fails. But maybe we can choose a different set of 5 non-sunny lines that cover 25 points leaving 3 collinear? Possibly using diagonals? For n=7, the maximum union from 5 lines is 25, but achieving that requires the lines to be all of the same type: horizontals or verticals. Those leave uncovered set of size 3 that is not collinear. Could there be a selection of 5 lines that cover 24 points, leaving 4 uncovered, and those 4 can be covered by 2 sunny lines? That seems plausible. But the problem asks to determine all k for given n. So we need a full classification.

Given the complexity, it might be that the answer is: k=0,1,2,3 are possible for all n≥5, and for n=3,4, k=0,1,3 for n=4 and k=0,1 for n=3. But we need to check n=6: we have k=0,1,3 possible? k=2 fails, so that would be a gap. So the set of achievable k depends on n.
